🔑:2. sqrt{2}.From the given and the Law of Sines, we havebegin{array}{l}sqrt{2} a=a sin A cdot sin B+bleft(1-sin ^{2} Aright) =b+sin A(a sin B-b sin A)=b . text { Therefore, } frac{b}{a}=sqrt{2} .end{array}

❓:A triangle of sides frac{3}{2}, frac{sqrt{5}}{2}, sqrt{2} is folded along a variable line perpendicular to the side of frac{3}{2}. Find the maximum value of the coincident area.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. I need to find the maximum coincident area when a triangle with sides 3/2, sqrt(5)/2, and sqrt(2) is folded along a variable line that's perpendicular to the side of length 3/2. Hmm, first, I should visualize the triangle. Let me sketch it out mentally. The triangle has sides 3/2, sqrt(5)/2, and sqrt(2). Let me check if it's a right triangle. Maybe that could help.Wait, let me compute the squares of the sides. The longest side is 3/2, which is 1.5. Let's square all sides:(3/2)^2 = 9/4 = 2.25,(sqrt(5)/2)^2 = 5/4 = 1.25,(sqrt(2))^2 = 2.So, 2.25, 1.25, and 2. Do these satisfy the Pythagorean theorem? Let's see: 1.25 + 2 = 3.25, which isn't 2.25. So no, not a right triangle. Hmm. Maybe I should determine which sides are opposite which angles? But perhaps that's not necessary right now.The problem mentions folding along a variable line perpendicular to the side of length 3/2. Let's call that side the base. So the base is 3/2 units long. The folding line is perpendicular to this base, so it's a vertical line if we imagine the base lying horizontally. Since the line is variable, it can be moved along the base. When we fold the triangle along this line, the part of the triangle on one side of the line is reflected over to the other side, and the coincident area is the overlapping region where the original and folded parts overlap. The question is to find the maximum value of this coincident area.Alright, so to model this, let's set up a coordinate system. Let me place the base of length 3/2 along the x-axis from point A(0,0) to point B(3/2, 0). The folding line is perpendicular to AB, so it's a vertical line x = t, where t ranges from 0 to 3/2. When we fold the triangle over this line x = t, the part of the triangle to the right of x = t (if t is less than 3/2) will be reflected over to the left, and vice versa. However, since the line is variable, we need to consider t moving from 0 to 3/2 and find the t that maximizes the overlapping area.But first, I need to know the coordinates of the third vertex of the triangle. Let's call it C. The triangle has sides AB = 3/2, AC, and BC. Wait, but the sides are given as 3/2, sqrt(5)/2, and sqrt(2). Let me confirm which sides correspond to which.Wait, in the problem statement, the sides are given as 3/2, sqrt(5)/2, sqrt(2). Let me denote AB as the base with length 3/2. Then the other two sides are AC and BC. So AC and BC would be sqrt(5)/2 and sqrt(2). Wait, but which is which?To find the coordinates of point C, we can use the distances from points A and B. Let's suppose that point C has coordinates (x, y). Then, the distance from A(0,0) to C(x,y) is sqrt(x^2 + y^2), and the distance from B(3/2, 0) to C(x,y) is sqrt((x - 3/2)^2 + y^2).Given that the sides are 3/2, sqrt(5)/2, and sqrt(2). Assuming AB is 3/2, then the other two sides must be AC and BC. Let's check which is which. Let's denote AC = sqrt(5)/2 and BC = sqrt(2). Alternatively, it could be the other way around. Let me check both possibilities.First, suppose AC = sqrt(5)/2 and BC = sqrt(2). Then:sqrt(x^2 + y^2) = sqrt(5)/2,sqrt((x - 3/2)^2 + y^2) = sqrt(2).Let me square both equations:x^2 + y^2 = 5/4,(x - 3/2)^2 + y^2 = 2.Subtract the first equation from the second:(x - 3/2)^2 + y^2 - x^2 - y^2 = 2 - 5/4,Expanding (x - 3/2)^2: x^2 - 3x + 9/4,So x^2 - 3x + 9/4 - x^2 = 2 - 5/4,Simplify left side: -3x + 9/4,Right side: 8/4 - 5/4 = 3/4,So -3x + 9/4 = 3/4,Subtract 9/4: -3x = 3/4 - 9/4 = -6/4 = -3/2,Divide by -3: x = (-3/2)/(-3) = 1/2.So x = 1/2. The vertices are (A(0,0)), (Bleft(frac{3}{2}, 0right)), and (C(1,1)).The folding line is vertical, given by (x = t), where (t) ranges from 0 to (frac{3}{2}). When folding along this line, the left part of the triangle (to the left of (x = t)) is reflected over to the right side. The coincident area is the overlapping region between the original triangle and the reflected part.Key steps:1. Reflection of point (C): The reflection of (C(1,1)) over (x = t) is (C'(2t - 1, 1)).2. Intersection analysis: The reflected triangle's edges intersect with the original triangle's edges. The equations of the edges are derived and their intersections are calculated.3. Area calculation: The area of the overlapping region is computed by integrating the appropriate functions over the interval where they overlap.After detailed analysis and calculus, it is found that the maximum coincident area occurs at (t = frac{6}{7}). The area is calculated by integrating the overlapping regions and results in the maximum area being (frac{9}{28}).[boxed{dfrac{9}{28}}]]

🔑:1. Identify the vertices and sides of the triangle: Let the vertices of the triangle be ( A, B, ) and ( C ) such that: [ |AB| = frac{3}{2}, quad |AC| = frac{sqrt{5}}{2}, quad |BC| = sqrt{2} ]2. Calculate the area of the original triangle: Using Heron's formula, we first find the semi-perimeter ( s ): [ s = frac{1}{2} left( frac{3}{2} + frac{sqrt{5}}{2} + sqrt{2} right) = frac{3 + sqrt{5} + 2sqrt{2}}{4} ] The area ( Delta ) of the triangle is given by: [ Delta = sqrt{s(s - a)(s - b)(s - c)} ] where ( a = frac{3}{2}, b = frac{sqrt{5}}{2}, c = sqrt{2} ). Substituting these values, we get: [ Delta = sqrt{left( frac{3 + sqrt{5} + 2sqrt{2}}{4} right) left( frac{3 + sqrt{5} + 2sqrt{2}}{4} - frac{3}{2} right) left( frac{3 + sqrt{5} + 2sqrt{2}}{4} - frac{sqrt{5}}{2} right) left( frac{3 + sqrt{5} + 2sqrt{2}}{4} - sqrt{2} right)} ] Simplifying the terms inside the square root, we find: [ Delta = frac{1}{4} ]3. Determine the height of the triangle relative to the base ( AB ): The height ( h ) from vertex ( C ) to the base ( AB ) can be calculated using the area formula: [ Delta = frac{1}{2} times text{base} times text{height} implies frac{1}{4} = frac{1}{2} times frac{3}{2} times h implies h = 1 ]4. Analyze the folding process: When the triangle is folded along a line perpendicular to ( AB ), the coincident area ( S ) changes. Initially, when the folding line passes through vertex ( C ), the coincident area ( S_0 ) is: [ S_0 = frac{1}{4} ]5. Displace the folding line by ( x ) towards vertex ( B ): As the folding line is displaced by ( x ), the coincident area ( S ) is affected by the areas of the trapezoid and the right triangle formed. The coincident area ( S ) can be expressed as: [ S = frac{1}{4} + x - frac{x^2}{2} - 3x^2 ]6. Find the maximum value of ( S ): To find the maximum value of ( S ), we take the derivative of ( S ) with respect to ( x ) and set it to zero: [ frac{dS}{dx} = 1 - x - 6x = 1 - 7x = 0 implies x = frac{1}{7} ] Substituting ( x = frac{1}{7} ) back into the expression for ( S ): [ S = frac{1}{4} + frac{1}{7} - frac{left( frac{1}{7} right)^2}{2} - 3 left( frac{1}{7} right)^2 = frac{1}{4} + frac{1}{7} - frac{1}{98} - frac{3}{49} ] Simplifying the terms, we get: [ S = frac{1}{4} + frac{1}{7} - frac{1}{98} - frac{6}{98} = frac{1}{4} + frac{1}{7} - frac{7}{98} = frac{1}{4} + frac{1}{7} - frac{1}{14} = frac{9}{28} ]The final answer is (boxed{frac{9}{28}})

The triangle and the circle have exactly one common point, which lies on the inscribed circle of the triangle.1. Distance between the lines: The circle of radius 1 touches both lines, so the distance between the lines is 2 units.2. Height of the triangle: The height of the isosceles triangle is equal to the distance between the lines, which is 2 units.3. Inradius formula: For an isosceles triangle with base (2a), equal sides (b), and height 2, the inradius (r) is given by (r = frac{2a}{a + b}) where (b = sqrt{a^2 + 4}).4. Tangency condition: The circle of radius 1 and the triangle have exactly one common point, which is on the inscribed circle. This implies the circle of radius 1 is tangent to the triangle at a point where the inscribed circle is tangent to the triangle.5. Distance between centers: The distance between the centers of the inscribed circle (radius (r)) and the circle of radius 1 is (r + 1). The center of the inscribed circle is ((0, r)) and the center of the circle of radius 1 is ((h, 1)). This distance is given by (sqrt{h^2 + (1 - r)^2} = r + 1).6. Solving for (h): Using the relationship (h = frac{2(1 - r)}{a}) and substituting into the distance equation, we find (h = 2sqrt{r}).7. Substituting (a): Expressing (a) in terms of (r) using the inradius formula and solving, we find (a = frac{r}{sqrt{1 - r}}).8. Let's compute:4r/t=4*(1/2 )/(3√2 )=2/(3√2 )=√2/3r +br/t=1/2 + (√2 )(1/2 )/(3√2 )=1/2 + (1/2 )/3=1/2 +1/6=2/3So the point is (√2/3,2/3 ). The original circle is centered at (√2,1 ) with radius1. The distance between (√2,1 ) and (√2/3,2/3 ) is sqrt( (√2 -√2/3 )² + (1 -2/3 )² )=sqrt( (2√2/3 )² + (1/3 )² )=sqrt(8/9 +1/9 )=sqrt(9/9 )=1. Correct. Therefore, the point is on the original circle. The slope between centers is (2/3 -1 ) / (√2/3 -√2 )= (-1/3 ) / (-2√2 /3 )= (1/3 ) / (2√2 /3 )=1/(2√2 )=√2/4. The slope of the leg is -4/b =-4/√2=-2√2. The slope of the perpendicular is b/4=√2/4. Which matches the slope between the centers. Therefore, the original circle is indeed tangent to the leg at that point. Therefore, all conditions are satisfied. Thus, the inradius is 1/2.Final Answerboxed{dfrac{1}{2}}</think>Given two parallel lines with a circle of radius 1 placed between them, touching both lines, and an isosceles triangle with its base on one line and vertex on the other. The circle and the triangle have exactly one common point, which lies on the inscribed circle of the triangle. We need to find the radius of the inscribed circle of the triangle.1. Setup and Key Observations: - The distance between the parallel lines is 2 units (twice the radius of the circle). - The height of the isosceles triangle is 2 units. - The inscribed circle (incircle) of the triangle is tangent to all three sides of the triangle. - The circle of radius 1 is tangent to both lines and touches the triangle at exactly one point, which is on the incircle of the triangle.2. Coordinates and Geometry: - The circle (radius 1) is centered at ((h, 1)) and touches both lines (y=0) and (y=2). - The triangle's base is on (y=0) and vertex at ((0, 2)). The incircle of the triangle is centered at ((0, r)) with radius (r).3. Key Equations and Relationships: - The inradius (r) of the triangle is given by (r = frac{A}{s}), where (A) is the area and (s) is the semiperimeter. - The area (A) of the triangle is (b) (base (b), height 2). - The semiperimeter (s) is (frac{b + 2s}{2}), where (s) is the length of the equal sides. - Using the Pythagorean theorem, the equal sides (s = sqrt{left(frac{b}{2}right)^2 + 4}).4. Tangency Conditions: - The circle and the incircle are tangent to each other at the common point. - The distance between the centers of the circle and the incircle is ( sqrt{h^2 + (1 - r)^2} ). - This distance must equal (1 + r) (externally tangent).5. Solving for (r): - From the tangency condition, we derive the equation ( sqrt{h^2 + (1 - r)^2} = 1 + r ). - Solving this, we find ( h = 2sqrt{r} ). - Using the relationship between (h), (b), and (r), we find ( b = frac{2(1 - r)}{sqrt{r}} ). - Substituting back, we solve for (r) and find ( r = frac{1}{2} ).Thus, the radius of the inscribed circle of the triangle is (boxed{dfrac{1}{2}}).]

❓:Given that {a_{n}} is an arithmetic sequence, {b_{n}} is a monotonically increasing geometric sequence, a_{1}=b_{1}=1, a_{2}+a_{4}=6, a_{3}b_{3}=12. (1) Find the general formulas for {a_{n}} and {b_{n}}. (2) Find the sum S_{n} of the first n terms of the sequence {a_{n}+b_{n}}.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's tackle this problem step by step. So, we have an arithmetic sequence {aₙ} and a geometric sequence {bₙ} that's monotonically increasing. Both start with 1, so a₁ = b₁ = 1. We need to find their general formulas and then the sum of the first n terms of the sequence {aₙ + bₙ}.Starting with part (1). For the arithmetic sequence {aₙ}, since it's arithmetic, each term is the previous term plus a common difference, let's call it d. So, the general formula for the nth term of an arithmetic sequence is aₙ = a₁ + (n - 1)d. Since a₁ is 1, that simplifies to aₙ = 1 + (n - 1)d.We are given that a₂ + a₄ = 6. Let's write out those terms using the formula:a₂ = 1 + (2 - 1)d = 1 + da₄ = 1 + (4 - 1)d = 1 + 3dAdding them together: (1 + d) + (1 + 3d) = 2 + 4d = 6So, solving for d:2 + 4d = 6Subtract 2 from both sides: 4d = 4Divide both sides by 4: d = 1So, the common difference d is 1. Therefore, the arithmetic sequence {aₙ} is:aₙ = 1 + (n - 1)*1 = 1 + n - 1 = nWait, that simplifies to aₙ = n? Let me check. If n=1, a₁=1, which is correct. n=2, a₂=2, which would be 1 + 1, yes. Then a₃=3, etc. So, the arithmetic sequence is just the natural numbers. That seems right because the difference is 1. Okay, that's straightforward.Now, moving on to the geometric sequence {bₙ}. It's a geometric sequence, so each term is the previous term multiplied by a common ratio, let's call it r. Since it's monotonically increasing, the common ratio must be greater than 1, right? Because if it were between 0 and 1, the terms would be decreasing, and if r = 1, it's constant. But since it's monotonically increasing, r > 1. 🔑: Solution:# Part (1): Finding the General FormulasLet's denote the common difference of the arithmetic sequence {a_{n}} as d and the common ratio of the geometric sequence {b_{n}} as q.- From the given a_{2}+a_{4}=6, we can express this in terms of d: [ a_{2}+a_{4} = (a_{1}+d) + (a_{1}+3d) = 2a_{1}+4d = 2(1) + 4d = 6 ] Simplifying, we find 4d = 4, which gives us d = 1.- Therefore, the general formula for {a_{n}}, using a_{n} = a_{1} + (n-1)d, becomes: [ a_{n} = 1 + (n-1)cdot1 = n ]- Given a_{3}b_{3}=12 and knowing a_{3}=3, we can solve for b_{3}: [ 3b_{3} = 12 implies b_{3} = 4 ] Since b_{1}=1, we can find q by using b_{3} = b_{1}q^{2}, which gives us: [ 4 = 1cdot q^{2} implies q^{2} = 4 ] Given that {b_{n}} is monotonically increasing and q > 0, we conclude q = 2.- Thus, the general formula for {b_{n}}, using b_{n} = b_{1}q^{n-1}, becomes: [ b_{n} = 1cdot2^{n-1} = 2^{n-1} ]So, the general formulas are a_{n} = n and b_{n} = 2^{n-1}.# Part (2): Finding the Sum S_{n}Given the general formulas for {a_{n}} and {b_{n}}, the sum of the first n terms of the sequence {a_{n}+b_{n}} can be found as follows:- The sum S_{n} is: [ S_{n} = sum_{i=1}^{n} (a_{i} + b_{i}) = sum_{i=1}^{n} i + sum_{i=1}^{n} 2^{i-1} ]- The sum of the first n natural numbers is frac{n(n+1)}{2}, and the sum of the first n terms of a geometric sequence with the first term 1 and common ratio 2 is frac{1-2^{n}}{1-2}.- Combining these, we get: [ S_{n} = frac{n(n+1)}{2} + frac{1-2^{n}}{1-2} = frac{1}{2}n(n+1) + 2^{n} - 1 ]Therefore, the sum of the first n terms of the sequence {a_{n}+b_{n}} is boxed{frac{1}{2}n(n+1) + 2^{n} - 1}.

❓:13. Given the hyperbola frac{x^{2}}{4}-frac{y^{2}}{3}=1, the endpoints of the real axis are A_{1} and A_{2}, and P is a moving point on the hyperbola different from points A_{1} and A_{2}. The lines P A_{1} and P A_{2} intersect the line x=1 at points M_{1} and M_{2}, respectively. Prove: The circle with diameter M_{1} M_{2} must pass through a fixed point.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. I need to prove that the circle with diameter M1M2 passes through a fixed point, regardless of where P is on the hyperbola. The hyperbola given is x²/4 - y²/3 = 1. The endpoints of the real axis are A1 and A2. Since it's a standard hyperbola centered at the origin, opening left and right, the real axis is the x-axis. The endpoints should be at (±a, 0), where a² = 4, so a = 2. Therefore, A1 is (-2, 0) and A2 is (2, 0). P is a moving point on the hyperbola, not equal to A1 or A2. Then, lines PA1 and PA2 intersect the line x=1 at points M1 and M2, respectively. We need to show that the circle with diameter M1M2 passes through a fixed point. First, let's visualize this. The hyperbola is x²/4 - y²/3 = 1. The line x=1 is a vertical line to the right of the center. Points M1 and M2 are where lines from P to A1 and P to A2 intersect x=1. The circle with diameter M1M2 should pass through some fixed point no matter where P is on the hyperbola. So, the strategy is probably to parametrize point P on the hyperbola, find equations for lines PA1 and PA2, find their intersection points M1 and M2 with x=1, then find the equation of the circle with diameter M1M2, and show that this circle always passes through a particular point, regardless of the parameter. Let's start by parametrizing point P. For hyperbolas, a common parametrization is using hyperbolic functions: x = a secθ, y = b tanθ. Here, a²=4 so a=2, b²=3 so b=√3. Therefore, parametrizing P as (2 secθ, √3 tanθ). This should satisfy the hyperbola equation:( (2 secθ)^2 ) /4 - ( (√3 tanθ)^2 ) /3 = (4 sec²θ)/4 - (3 tan²θ)/3 = sec²θ - tan²θ = 1, which checks out because sec²θ - tan²θ = 1.So, point P can be written as (2 secθ, √3 tanθ). Now, we need to find equations for lines PA1 and PA2. Let's do PA1 first. A1 is (-2, 0). So, the line PA1 connects (-2, 0) to (2 secθ, √3 tanθ). Let's find the equation of this line.The slope of PA1 is (√3 tanθ - 0)/(2 secθ - (-2)) = (√3 tanθ)/(2 secθ + 2) = (√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ + 1)).We can write the equation using point-slope form. Let's use point A1 (-2, 0):y - 0 = [√3 tanθ / (2(secθ + 1))](x + 2)Similarly, for PA2, which connects A2 (2, 0) to P (2 secθ, √3 tanθ). The slope is (√3 tanθ - 0)/(2 secθ - 2) = (√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ - 1)).Equation using point A2 (2, 0):y - 0 = [√3 tanθ / (2(secθ - 1))](x - 2)Now, we need to find points M1 and M2 where these lines intersect x=1. Let's find M1 first by plugging x=1 into PA1's equation:y = [√3 tanθ / (2(secθ + 1))](1 + 2) = [√3 tanθ / (2(secθ + 1))]*(3) = (3√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ + 1))So, M1 is (1, (3√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ + 1)))Similarly, find M2 by plugging x=1 into PA2's equation:y = [√3 tanθ / (2(secθ - 1))](1 - 2) = [√3 tanθ / (2(secθ - 1))]*(-1) = (-√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ - 1))So, M2 is (1, (-√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ - 1)))Now, we have coordinates for M1 and M2. The next step is to write the equation of the circle with diameter M1M2. The general equation of a circle with diameter endpoints (x1, y1) and (x2, y2) is (x - x1)(x - x2) + (y - y1)(y - y2) = 0.But here, both M1 and M2 are on the line x=1, so their x-coordinates are both 1. So, M1 is (1, y1) and M2 is (1, y2). Therefore, the circle equation becomes:(x - 1)(x - 1) + (y - y1)(y - y2) = 0Which simplifies to:(x - 1)^2 + (y - y1)(y - y2) = 0So, expanding this, we have x² - 2x + 1 + y² - (y1 + y2)y + y1y2 = 0Alternatively, x² + y² - 2x + 1 - (y1 + y2)y + y1y2 = 0Our goal is to show that this circle passes through a fixed point, regardless of θ. So, if we can find a point (h, k) that satisfies this equation for all θ, then that's the fixed point.Alternatively, perhaps substitute y1 and y2 expressions in terms of θ, then analyze the equation.Let me compute y1 and y2.From above, y1 = (3√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ + 1))Similarly, y2 = (-√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ - 1))So, let's compute y1 + y2 and y1 y2.First, let's express y1 and y2 in terms of sinθ and cosθ.Recall that secθ = 1/cosθ, tanθ = sinθ/cosθ.So, let's rewrite y1:y1 = (3√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ + 1)) = (3√3 (sinθ/cosθ))/(2(1/cosθ + 1)) = (3√3 sinθ / cosθ) / (2( (1 + cosθ)/cosθ )) = (3√3 sinθ / cosθ ) * (cosθ / (2(1 + cosθ))) ) = 3√3 sinθ / (2(1 + cosθ))Similarly, y2 = (-√3 tanθ)/(2(secθ - 1)) = (-√3 (sinθ/cosθ))/(2(1/cosθ - 1)) = (-√3 sinθ / cosθ) / (2( (1 - cosθ)/cosθ )) = (-√3 sinθ / cosθ ) * (cosθ / (2(1 - cosθ))) ) = (-√3 sinθ ) / (2(1 - cosθ))So, now y1 = 3√3 sinθ / [2(1 + cosθ)]y2 = -√3 sinθ / [2(1 - cosθ)]Let me compute y1 + y2:3√3 sinθ / [2(1 + cosθ)] - √3 sinθ / [2(1 - cosθ)]Factor out √3 sinθ / 2:√3 sinθ / 2 [3/(1 + cosθ) - 1/(1 - cosθ)]Combine the fractions:3/(1 + cosθ) - 1/(1 - cosθ) = [3(1 - cosθ) - (1 + cosθ)] / [(1 + cosθ)(1 - cosθ)] = [3 - 3 cosθ -1 - cosθ]/[1 - cos²θ] = [2 - 4 cosθ]/sin²θTherefore, y1 + y2 = √3 sinθ / 2 * (2 - 4 cosθ)/sin²θ = √3 (2 - 4 cosθ)/(2 sinθ) = √3 (1 - 2 cosθ)/sinθSimilarly, compute y1 y2:[3√3 sinθ / (2(1 + cosθ))] * [ -√3 sinθ / (2(1 - cosθ)) ] = -3*3 sin²θ / [4(1 + cosθ)(1 - cosθ)] = -9 sin²θ / [4(1 - cos²θ)] = -9 sin²θ / [4 sin²θ] = -9/4Because 1 - cos²θ = sin²θ. A moving point (P) on the hyperbola different from (A_1) and (A_2) is considered. The lines (PA_1) and (PA_2) intersect the line (x = 1) at points (M_1) and (M_2), respectively. We need to prove that the circle with diameter (M_1M_2) must pass through a fixed point.1. Parametrization of Point (P): - Using hyperbolic functions, (P) can be parametrized as ((2 sec theta, sqrt{3} tan theta)).2. Equations of Lines (PA_1) and (PA_2): - The line (PA_1) has the equation (y = frac{sqrt{3} tan theta}{2 (sec theta + 1)} (x + 2)). - The line (PA_2) has the equation (y = frac{sqrt{3} tan theta}{2 (sec theta - 1)} (x - 2)).3. Finding Intersection Points (M_1) and (M_2): - For (M_1) (intersection of (PA_1) with (x = 1)): [ y = frac{3 sqrt{3} tan theta}{2 (sec theta + 1)} ] - For (M_2) (intersection of (PA_2) with (x = 1)): [ y = frac{-sqrt{3} tan theta}{2 (sec theta - 1)} ]4. Midpoint and Radius of the Circle: - Midpoint of (M_1M_2) is ((1, frac{sqrt{3} (sec theta - 2)}{2 tan theta})). - Radius is half the distance between (M_1) and (M_2).5. Circle Equation: - The circle equation is derived and simplified to show that it must pass through the fixed points ((- frac{1}{2}, 0)) and ((frac{5}{2}, 0)) regardless of (theta).6. Verification with Examples: - Specific examples and limit cases confirm that the circle passes through both fixed points for any (P) on the hyperbola.Conclusion:The circle with diameter (M_1M_2) must pass through the fixed points (boxed{left( -dfrac{1}{2}, 0 right)}) and (boxed{left( dfrac{5}{2}, 0 right)}).]

🔑:Three, 13. Given, let A_{1}(-2,0), A_{2}(2,0), and a moving point Pleft(x_{0}, y_{0}right) on the hyperbola with y_{0} neq 0. Thus,frac{x_{0}^{2}}{4}-frac{y_{0}^{2}}{3}=1.Also, l_{P A_{1}}: y=frac{y_{0}}{x_{0}+2}(x+2),l_{P A_{2}}: y=frac{y_{0}}{x_{0}-2}(x-2),then M_{1}left(1, frac{3 y_{0}}{x_{0}+2}right), M_{2}left(1, frac{-y_{0}}{x_{0}-2}right).For any point Q(x, y) on the circle Gamma with diameter M_{1} M_{2}, by overrightarrow{M_{1} Q} cdot overrightarrow{M_{2} Q}=0, we knowthe circle Gamma :(x-1)(x-1)+left(y-frac{3 y_{0}}{x_{0}+2}right)left(y-frac{-y_{0}}{x_{0}-2}right)=0.Let y=0, substituting into the above equation gives(x-1)^{2}-frac{3 y_{0}^{2}}{x_{0}^{2}-4}=0.From frac{x_{0}^{2}}{4}-frac{y_{0}^{2}}{3}=1 Rightarrow frac{y_{0}^{2}}{x_{0}^{2}-4}=frac{3}{4}.Thus, (x-1)^{2}-frac{9}{4}=0 Rightarrow x=frac{5}{2} or x=-frac{1}{2}.Therefore, the circle with diameter M_{1} M_{2} must pass through the two fixed points left(-frac{1}{2}, 0right),left(frac{5}{2}, 0right).
